We provide approximation algorithms andinapproximability results for the Shift-Bribery problem,introduced by Elkind et al. [EFS09] to capturethe idea of campaigning in elections. Briefly put, we are given anelection where each voter ranks the candidates from the most to theleast appealing one, and our goal is to ensure that a given preferredcandidate becomes the winner. To this end, we can shift this candidateup within the voters’ preference orders, but each such shift comeswith a price (which, for example, measures the difficulty ofconvincing the voter that our candidate is more appealing than thevoter originally thought). Naturally, we are interested in finding ascheap a solution aspossible.

While the Shift-Bribery problem was introduced in the contextof buying votes and in the context of campaigning, bribery problemshave a number of other applications (see, e.g., the works on themargin of victory problem [MRS11, Car11, Xia12] and on measuringcandidate success [FST17]; see also the original paper ofFaliszewski et al. [FHH09] and the survey ofFaliszewski and Rothe [FR16]). For example, a Formula 1 seasonconsists of about 20 races, where each race can be seen as a voterranking the candidates (the drivers) in the order in which theyfinished the race. For each finishing position, there is an associatednumber of points and the driver who collects most points becomes theworld champion (i.e., this “election” uses a positionalscoring rule as a voting rule). We can use theShift-Bribery problem to measure how close each driver was towinning the world championship. For example, we can set the price forshifting a driver up by some t𝑡t positions in a given race to be thedifference between the finishing times of the driver and whoeverranked t𝑡t positions higher. Then, the cheapest shift briberycorresponds to the smallest speed-up that the driver needed to becomethe world champion. As argued by Faliszewski et al. [FST17], suchvalues can be far more informative than the score differences betweenthe drivers. Bribery problems also appear in the contexts oflobbying [BEF+07, BEF+14], rating systems [GST18], or incombinatorial domains [BEER15].

With the exception of a few simple voting rules, such as thek𝑘k-Approval family of rules and the Bucklin rule,Shift-Bribery tends to be NPNP{{\mathrm{NP}}}-hard (see the works of Elkindet al. [EFS09] and Schlotter et al. [SFE17]). Indeed, thisis the case, e.g., for Borda, Copeland, Maximin [EFS09] andvarious elimination-based rules [MNRS18]. Yet, in many cases itcan be solved quite effectively. For example, for the case of Bordathere is a polynomial-time 2-approximation algorithm of Elkind etal. [EFS09, EF10] and several FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} algorithms of Bredereck etal. [BCF+16]. On the other hand, for the case of Copeland,Shift-Bribery is W​[1]Wdelimited-[]1{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]-hard for many naturalparameters [BCF+16]111One notable exception is theparameterization by the number of candidates [KKM17]. and thebest known polynomial-time approximation algorithm has linearapproximation ratio [EF10].

In fact, the difference between the Borda rule (and, in general, thepositional scoring rules) and the Copeland rule is even morestriking. We show that the former can be solved nearly perfectly inpolynomial time, whereas for the latter we give stronginapproximability results:

1.Our main contribution is the first polynomial-time approximationscheme (PTAS) for Shift-Bribery for positional scoringrules (Theorem 12).In fact, our algorithm works even for the case where thescoring vectors are different for different voters. Our algorithmuses linear programming and, in particular, basic solutions oflinear programs. For the case of unit prices (i.e., for the casewhere each unit shift has the same cost) we even obtain an EPTAS,i.e., a PTAS for which the non-polynomial factors in the runningtime depend on the approximation ratioonly (Theorem 7).We also show a simple combinatorial PTAS for this case(Theorem 4).2.For the case of the Copeland rule, we give a reduction thatpreserves approximation ratios up to some polynomial from theDensest k𝑘k-Subgraph (Dk𝑘kS) problem toShift-Bribery (Theorem 14).Since it is generally believed that Densestk𝑘k-Subgraph is hard to approximate up to a polynomialfactor [BCV+12, Man17], the same beliefs transfer to the caseof Copeland-Shift-Bribery. In particular, this gives analmost-polynomial ratio hardness of approximatingCopeland-Shift-Bribery under the ETH and Gap-ETH assumptions(Corollary 15).We also show that under Gap-ETH, Copeland-Shift-Briberydoes not admit an FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} approximation scheme for theparameterization by the number of unit shifts(Theorem 16) or by the number of affectedvoters (Theorem 17), even for thecase of unit prices. This is in contrast to parameterization by thenumber of voters or by the number of candidates, for which FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}}approximation schemes are known to exist [BCF+16].

Together with the results of Elkind et al. [EFS09, EF10]and Bredereck etal. [BCF+16], our work gives a nearly complete view of thecomplexity and approximability of Shift-Bribery forpositional scoring rules and the Copeland rule.

A voting rule ℛℛ\mathcal{R} is a function that for each electionE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}) outputs the setℛ​(E)⊆Cℛ𝐸𝐶\mathcal{R}(E)\subseteq C of this election’s tied winners. We focus on theclass of positional scoring rules and on the Copeland rule.

Consider a setting with m𝑚m candidates. Under a positionalscoring rule ℛ𝐰subscriptℛ𝐰\mathcal{R}_{\mathbf{w}}, we have a vector𝐰=(w1,…,wm)∈ℝm𝐰subscript𝑤1…subscript𝑤𝑚superscriptℝ𝑚\mathbf{w}=(w_{1},\ldots,w_{m})\in\mathbb{R}^{m} of point values associated withthe candidate positions in the preference orders.Each voter gives each candidate the number of points associated withthis candidate’s position, and the candidates with the highest totalscore are the winners. For example, the Plurality rule uses vectorsof the form (1,0,…,0)10…0(1,0,\ldots,0), the k𝑘k-Approval rule uses vectorswith k𝑘k ones followed by m−k𝑚𝑘m-k zeros, and the Borda rule uses vectorsof the form (m−1,…,2,1,0)𝑚1…210(m-1,\ldots,2,1,0).

The Copeland rule is based on the idea of pairwise elections among thecandidates. Let E𝐸E be an election and let c,c′𝑐superscript𝑐′c,c^{\prime} be twocandidates. By NE​(c,c′)subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′N_{E}(c,c^{\prime}) we mean the number of voters who prefer c𝑐cover c′superscript𝑐′c^{\prime}, i.e., |Vc≻c′|subscript𝑉succeeds𝑐superscript𝑐′|V_{c\succ c^{\prime}}|.We say that a candidate c𝑐c wins pairwise electionagainst c′superscript𝑐′c^{\prime} if NE​(c,c′)>NE​(c′,c)subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′subscript𝑁𝐸superscript𝑐′𝑐N_{E}(c,c^{\prime})>N_{E}(c^{\prime},c). Similarly, we say that c𝑐cties (resp. loses) pairwise election against c′superscript𝑐′c^{\prime} ifNE​(c,c′)=NE​(c′,c)subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′subscript𝑁𝐸superscript𝑐′𝑐N_{E}(c,c^{\prime})=N_{E}(c^{\prime},c) (resp. NE​(c,c′)<NE​(c′,c)subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′subscript𝑁𝐸superscript𝑐′𝑐N_{E}(c,c^{\prime})<N_{E}(c^{\prime},c)).

For α∈[0,1]𝛼01\alpha\in[0,1], the CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha} rule assigns to eachcandidate c𝑐c one point for each candidate with whom c𝑐c wins apairwise election, and α𝛼\alpha points for each candidate with whomc𝑐c ties. Formally, each candidate c𝑐c receives|{c′∈C∖{c}:NE​(c,c′)>NE​(c′,c)}|+α​|{c′∈C∖{c}:NE​(c,c′)=NE​(c′,c)}|conditional-setsuperscript𝑐′𝐶𝑐subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′subscript𝑁𝐸superscript𝑐′𝑐𝛼conditional-setsuperscript𝑐′𝐶𝑐subscript𝑁𝐸𝑐superscript𝑐′subscript𝑁𝐸superscript𝑐′𝑐|\{c^{\prime}\in C\setminus\{c\}:N_{E}(c,c^{\prime})>N_{E}(c^{\prime},c)\}|+\alpha|\{c^{\prime}\in C\setminus\{c\}:N_{E}(c,c^{\prime})=N_{E}(c^{\prime},c)\}|points. The winners are all the candidates with the maximum score.

Let ℛℛ\mathcal{R} be a voting rule and let I𝐼I be a Shift-Briberyinstance with election E𝐸E and preferred candidate p𝑝p.Shift action 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} is successful for I𝐼I under ℛℛ\mathcal{R} if p𝑝p isan ℛℛ\mathcal{R}-winner in shift​(E,𝐬)shift𝐸𝐬\text{shift}(E,\mathbf{s}), i.e., ifp∈ℛ​(shift​(E,𝐬))𝑝ℛshift𝐸𝐬p\in\mathcal{R}(\text{shift}(E,\mathbf{s})). ℛℛ\mathcal{R}-Shift-Bribery isan optimization problem where, given a Shift-Briberyinstance I𝐼I, we ask for a successful shift action with the lowestcost. We write OPT(I)OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I) to denote this lowest cost.

There are two particularly interesting families of price functions. Aunit price function defines the cost of each unit shift to beone, i.e., if ψvsubscript𝜓𝑣\psi_{v} is a unit price function then ψv​(ℓ)=ℓsubscript𝜓𝑣ℓℓ\psi_{v}(\ell)=\ell for each legal shift value ℓℓ\ell. An all-or-nothingprice function is such that the cost of shifting the preferredcandidate is the same, irrespective by how many positions we shift himor her. Formally, if ψvsubscript𝜓𝑣\psi_{v} is an all-or-nothing price functionthen there is a value cvsubscript𝑐𝑣c_{v} such that ψv​(ℓ)=cvsubscript𝜓𝑣ℓsubscript𝑐𝑣\psi_{v}(\ell)=c_{v} for allpositive integers ℓℓ\ell that represent legal shifts (and, of course,ψv​(0)=0subscript𝜓𝑣00\psi_{v}(0)=0). An instance I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) has (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices if it has all-or-nothing pricefunctions and for each voter v𝑣v the value cvsubscript𝑐𝑣c_{v} is in {1,∞}1\{1,\infty\}. Given such an instance I𝐼I, we define its width tobe the maximum of πv−1​(p)−1subscriptsuperscript𝜋1𝑣𝑝1\pi^{-1}_{v}(p)-1 over all v∈V𝑣𝑉v\in V such thatcv=1subscript𝑐𝑣1c_{v}=1. In other words, it is the maximum number of unit shiftspossible to perform within a single vote by paying a unit ofprice. Another family of all-or-nothing prices that we will discuss isthe family of uniform-all-or-nothing prices, for which cv=1subscript𝑐𝑣1c_{v}=1 for all v∈V𝑣𝑉v\in V.

In the Linear Programming problem we are givenan m×n𝑚𝑛m\times n matrix A𝐴A, an m𝑚m-dimensionalcolumn vector b𝑏b, an n𝑛n-dimensional column vector c𝑐c, and we askfor an n𝑛n dimensional column vector x𝑥x that minimizes the valuecT​xsuperscript𝑐𝑇𝑥c^{T}x subject to the condition that A​x⩾b𝐴𝑥𝑏Ax\geqslant b.A basic solutionto such a problem is a solution x∈ℝn𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛x\in\mathbb{R}^{n} such that there are n𝑛nlinearly independent rows aisubscript𝑎𝑖a_{i} of A𝐴A with ai​x=bisubscript𝑎𝑖𝑥subscript𝑏𝑖a_{i}x=b_{i}. It is knownthat when {x∈ℝn:A​x⩾b}conditional-set𝑥superscriptℝ𝑛𝐴𝑥𝑏\{x\in\mathbb{R}^{n}:Ax\geqslant b\} is feasible and bounded, there always isa basic solution that achieves the optimum, and itcan becomputed up to an arbitrary error in polynomial time(see, e.g., [LRS11] for the use of basic solutions in approximationalgorithms).

We now move on to our results. We first show approximation schemes forthe case of the Borda rule, mostly focusing on the case of unitprices.We start with Borda because it is one of the simplest rules,for which we can present our ideas most clearly, and because it is a verypractical rule (in particular, relevant to various competitions).

We first define two values that will guide our algorithms, and we explaintheir usefulness.

The former value gives the score difference between the preferredcandidate and his or her strongest opponent, whereas the lattermeasures the total number of points that the non-preferred candidatesneed to lose, provided that the preferred one gains k𝑘k points.

Elkind et al. [EFS09] note that given an instance I𝐼I ofBorda-Shift-Bribery, if K𝐾K is the smallest number of unitshifts in an optimal solution, then𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2⩽K⩽𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2𝐾𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2\leqslant K\leqslant\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I). Indeed, if the preferredcandidate gains 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I) points then he or she certainly matcheshis or her strongest opponent. On the other hand, the preferredcandidate needs at least 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2 unit shifts because each ofthem decreases the score difference between him or her and thestrongest opponent by at most two. However, it turns out that𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k) provides an even more useful bound.

Proof.After applying 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s}, the score of p𝑝p is exactlyscE​(p)+k𝐬subscriptsc𝐸𝑝subscript𝑘𝐬\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+k_{\mathbf{s}}. Thus each candidate c∈C𝑐𝐶c\in C must have lostat least max⁡{0,scE​(c)−scE​(p)−k𝐬}0subscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝subscript𝑘𝐬\max\{0,\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k_{\mathbf{s}}\} points. We will also make use of the following subroutine,which is based on a simple dynamic program.(Note that it is not restricted to unit prices.)

Let the notation be as in the statement of the lemma, let m𝑚mbe the number of candidates in E𝐸E, and letV=(v1,…,vn)𝑉subscript𝑣1…subscript𝑣𝑛V=(v_{1},\ldots,v_{n}) be the collection of voters in E𝐸E.

We now give a simple combinatorial PTAS for Borda-Shift-Briberywith unit prices.The main idea of our algorithm is as follows. If the optimal numberof unit shifts needed is OPTOPT\operatorname*{OPT}, then, in total, the scores of othercandidates decrease by at most OPTOPT\operatorname*{OPT}. This means that, once we guessOPTOPT\operatorname*{OPT} correctly, for each ε>0𝜀0\varepsilon>0 there can be at most1/ε1𝜀1/\varepsilon “bad” candidates, whose scores exceed that of thepreferred candidate by more than (1+ε)​OPT1𝜀OPT(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}. Since thereare only 1/ε1𝜀1/\varepsilon such candidates, we can use the algorithmfrom Lemma 3 to compute the cheapest set of (at most)OPTOPT\operatorname*{OPT} unit shifts that ensure that the preferred candidate defeatsthese candidates. Then, we shift the preferred candidate up furtherε​OPT𝜀OPT\varepsilon\operatorname*{OPT} times, which ensures that p𝑝p also defeats all theother candidates. In total, we use only (1+ε)​OPT1𝜀OPT(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT} shiftsand hence we arrive at our PTAS for the unit prices case. This idea isformalized below.

Proof.Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery and let ε>0𝜀0\varepsilon>0 be thedesired approximation ratio.For every k𝑘k between 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2 and 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I), such that𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)⩽k𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘𝑘\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k)\leqslant k, weexecute the following steps:1.Let CBADk={c1,…,ct​(k)}superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘subscript𝑐1…subscript𝑐𝑡𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}=\{c_{1},\ldots,c_{t(k)}\} be the setof candidates whose scores are greater thanscE​(p)+(1+ε)​ksubscriptsc𝐸𝑝1𝜀𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+(1+\varepsilon)k. We use the algorithm from Lemma 3 to find the least-cost shift action thatdecreases the score of each c∈CBADk𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} by at leastscE​(c)−scE​(p)−ksubscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k points.2.If the cost of this shift action is at most k𝑘k, then weperform additional arbitrary unit shifts so that the total numberof unit shifts is ⌊(1+ε)​k⌋1𝜀𝑘\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor or, if notenough unit shifts are possible, we shift p𝑝pto the top of every vote.We output all the performed unit shifts andterminate.We first note that the algorithm indeed outputs a successful shiftaction. If p𝑝p ends up being on top of all the votes then he or sheclearly wins. On the other hand, if the total number of unit shiftsperformed is ⌊(1+ε)​k⌋1𝜀𝑘\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor, then the score ofp𝑝p is at least scE​(p)+⌊(1+ε)​k⌋subscriptsc𝐸𝑝1𝜀𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor;this means that, for all the candidates c∉CBADk𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘c\notin C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}, the newscore of p𝑝p is at least scE​(c)subscriptsc𝐸𝑐\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c), which is at least as large asthe score of c𝑐c after the shifts. Moreover, the algorithm fromLemma 3 ensures that after the shifts the score of p𝑝p isat least as high as the scores of all the candidates fromCBADksuperscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}. Thus, p𝑝p is a winner.Next, let us argue that the algorithm computes a(1+ε)1𝜀(1+\varepsilon)-approximate solution.Recall that due to the results of Elkind et al. [EFS09],the number of unit shifts in the optimalsolution is between 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2 and 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I).Therefore the algorithm mustterminate at latest when considering k=OPT(I)𝑘OPT𝐼k=\operatorname*{OPT}(I).Given this manyshifts, it is—by definition—possible for p𝑝p to obtain score higherthan all the candidates from CBADksuperscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} and, so, the algorithm fromLemma 3 returns a shift action with at most OPT(I)OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I) unitshifts. Thus the algorithm terminates with at most⌊(1+ε)​OPT(I)⌋1𝜀OPT𝐼\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\rfloor unit shifts.The running time of the algorithm follows from Lemma 3and is bounded by a polynomial in |I|𝐼|I| andΠc∈CBADk(scE(c)−scE(p)−k+1)⩽(𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓(I)+1)t​(k)=OPT(I)O​(t​(k))\Pi_{c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}}(\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k+1)\leqslant(\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)+1)^{t(k)}=\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(t(k))}.However, we only invokeLemma 3 when k⩾𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)𝑘𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘k\geqslant\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k). This means that:k⩾𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)=∑c∈Cmax⁡{0,scE​(c)−scE​(p)−k}⩾∑c∈CBADk(scE​(c)−scE​(p)−k)>t​(k)⋅ε​k,𝑘𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘subscript𝑐𝐶0subscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘subscript𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘subscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘⋅𝑡𝑘𝜀𝑘\displaystyle k\geqslant\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k)=\sum_{c\in C}\max\{0,\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k\}\geqslant\sum_{c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}}(\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k)>t(k)\cdot\varepsilon k,and we conclude that t​(k)<1/ε𝑡𝑘1𝜀t(k)<1/\varepsilon.Thus the running time ispolynomial with respect to |I|+OPT(I)O​(1/ε)|I|+\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(1/\varepsilon)}.□□\square

Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery and let ε>0𝜀0\varepsilon>0 be thedesired approximation ratio.For every k𝑘k between 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2 and 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I), such that𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)⩽k𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘𝑘\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k)\leqslant k, weexecute the following steps:1.Let CBADk={c1,…,ct​(k)}superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘subscript𝑐1…subscript𝑐𝑡𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}=\{c_{1},\ldots,c_{t(k)}\} be the setof candidates whose scores are greater thanscE​(p)+(1+ε)​ksubscriptsc𝐸𝑝1𝜀𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+(1+\varepsilon)k. We use the algorithm from Lemma 3 to find the least-cost shift action thatdecreases the score of each c∈CBADk𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} by at leastscE​(c)−scE​(p)−ksubscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k points.2.If the cost of this shift action is at most k𝑘k, then weperform additional arbitrary unit shifts so that the total numberof unit shifts is ⌊(1+ε)​k⌋1𝜀𝑘\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor or, if notenough unit shifts are possible, we shift p𝑝pto the top of every vote.We output all the performed unit shifts andterminate.

We first note that the algorithm indeed outputs a successful shiftaction. If p𝑝p ends up being on top of all the votes then he or sheclearly wins. On the other hand, if the total number of unit shiftsperformed is ⌊(1+ε)​k⌋1𝜀𝑘\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor, then the score ofp𝑝p is at least scE​(p)+⌊(1+ε)​k⌋subscriptsc𝐸𝑝1𝜀𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)k\rfloor;this means that, for all the candidates c∉CBADk𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘c\notin C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}, the newscore of p𝑝p is at least scE​(c)subscriptsc𝐸𝑐\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c), which is at least as large asthe score of c𝑐c after the shifts. Moreover, the algorithm fromLemma 3 ensures that after the shifts the score of p𝑝p isat least as high as the scores of all the candidates fromCBADksuperscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}. Thus, p𝑝p is a winner.

Next, let us argue that the algorithm computes a(1+ε)1𝜀(1+\varepsilon)-approximate solution.Recall that due to the results of Elkind et al. [EFS09],the number of unit shifts in the optimalsolution is between 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2 and 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I).Therefore the algorithm mustterminate at latest when considering k=OPT(I)𝑘OPT𝐼k=\operatorname*{OPT}(I).Given this manyshifts, it is—by definition—possible for p𝑝p to obtain score higherthan all the candidates from CBADksuperscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} and, so, the algorithm fromLemma 3 returns a shift action with at most OPT(I)OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I) unitshifts. Thus the algorithm terminates with at most⌊(1+ε)​OPT(I)⌋1𝜀OPT𝐼\lfloor(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\rfloor unit shifts.

The running time of the algorithm follows from Lemma 3and is bounded by a polynomial in |I|𝐼|I| andΠc∈CBADk(scE(c)−scE(p)−k+1)⩽(𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓(I)+1)t​(k)=OPT(I)O​(t​(k))\Pi_{c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}}(\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k+1)\leqslant(\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)+1)^{t(k)}=\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(t(k))}.However, we only invokeLemma 3 when k⩾𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)𝑘𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘k\geqslant\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k). This means that:k⩾𝑠𝑢𝑚​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I,k)=∑c∈Cmax⁡{0,scE​(c)−scE​(p)−k}⩾∑c∈CBADk(scE​(c)−scE​(p)−k)>t​(k)⋅ε​k,𝑘𝑠𝑢𝑚-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼𝑘subscript𝑐𝐶0subscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘subscript𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘subscriptsc𝐸𝑐subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘⋅𝑡𝑘𝜀𝑘\displaystyle k\geqslant\mathit{sum\hbox{-}diff}(I,k)=\sum_{c\in C}\max\{0,\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k\}\geqslant\sum_{c\in C_{\text{BAD}}^{k}}(\mathrm{sc}_{E}(c)-\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)-k)>t(k)\cdot\varepsilon k,and we conclude that t​(k)<1/ε𝑡𝑘1𝜀t(k)<1/\varepsilon.Thus the running time ispolynomial with respect to |I|+OPT(I)O​(1/ε)|I|+\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(1/\varepsilon)}.□□\square

Using a very similar reasoning as in Theorem 4,we obtain an FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} algorithm for Borda-Shift-Bribery (witharbitrary price functions) parameterized by the smallest number K𝐾K ofunit shifts in an optimal solution.While this case was already knownto be in FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}}, our algorithm runs in 2O​(K)​poly​(|I|)superscript2𝑂𝐾poly𝐼2^{O(K)}\text{poly}(|I|) time, whichis faster than the known 2O​(K2)​poly​(|I|)superscript2𝑂superscript𝐾2poly𝐼2^{O(K^{2})}\text{poly}(|I|)-time algorithm ofBredereck et al. [BCF+16].

The main result of this subsection is an EPTAS (efficientpolynomial-time approximation scheme) for Borda-Shift-Briberywith unit prices, that is, a PTAS for which the non-polynomial factorsin the running time depend only on the required approximationratio. Note that in the algorithm fromTheorem 4 this factor wasOPT(I)O​(1/ε)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(1/\varepsilon)} and, thus, did not depend onε𝜀\varepsilon alone.

On the technical level, we first develop an algorithm that for aninstance I𝐼I of Borda-Shift-Bribery with unit prices outputsa solution with cost at most OPT(I)+OPT(I)OPT𝐼OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+\sqrt{\operatorname*{OPT}(I)}.The general structure of our algorithm is similar to that of thealgorithm from Theorem 4, but instead of invokingLemma 3, we solve a linear program. We form this programin such a way that its basic solution has to consist almost entirelyof integral values. Then,rounding and complementing theobtained shift action with arbitrary unit shifts gives the desiredsolution.

To state our linear program, we will model shift actions as booleanmatrices (x(v,j))v∈V,j∈[m]subscriptsubscript𝑥𝑣𝑗formulae-sequence𝑣𝑉𝑗delimited-[]𝑚(x_{(v,j)})_{v\in V,j\in[m]} such that x(v,j)subscript𝑥𝑣𝑗x_{(v,j)} is111 if after applying the shift action the preferred candidate isranked on position j𝑗j or better in vote v𝑣v, and it is 00 otherwise(so we will always have0⩽x(v,1)⩽x(v,2)⩽⋯⩽x(v,m)⩽10subscript𝑥𝑣1subscript𝑥𝑣2⋯subscript𝑥𝑣𝑚10\leqslant x_{(v,1)}\leqslant x_{(v,2)}\leqslant\cdots\leqslant x_{(v,m)}\leqslant~{}1).Formally, a shift action 𝐬=(sv)v∈V𝐬subscriptsubscript𝑠𝑣𝑣𝑉\mathbf{s}=(s_{v})_{v\in V} corresponds to theboolean matrix x(v,j)=𝟙​[πv−1​(p)−sv⩽j]subscript𝑥𝑣𝑗1delimited-[]superscriptsubscript𝜋𝑣1𝑝subscript𝑠𝑣𝑗x_{(v,j)}=\mathds{1}[\pi_{v}^{-1}(p)-s_{v}\leqslant j].

Proof.Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery with unit prices, whereE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}). Let(x(v,j)OPT)v∈V,j∈[πv−1​(p)−1]subscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑥OPT𝑣𝑗formulae-sequence𝑣𝑉𝑗delimited-[]subscriptsuperscript𝜋1𝑣𝑝1(x^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{(v,j)})_{v\in V,j\in[\pi^{-1}_{v}(p)-1]} be thebasic optimal solution found.3.Let 𝐬𝐿𝑃superscript𝐬𝐿𝑃\mathbf{s}^{\mathit{LP}} be the shift action corresponding to(⌊x(v,j)OPT⌋)v∈V,j∈[πv−1​(p)−1]subscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑥OPT𝑣𝑗formulae-sequence𝑣𝑉𝑗delimited-[]subscriptsuperscript𝜋1𝑣𝑝1(\lfloor x^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{(v,j)}\rfloor)_{v\in V,j\in[\pi^{-1}_{v}(p)-1]}.(Note that the rounded solution indeed correctly describes a shiftaction and that its cost, i.e., the number of unit shifts itcontains, is at most k𝑘k.) If p𝑝p ends up at the top of every preference order,then surely 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} is a successful shift action. For each integral voterv𝑣v, exactly πv−1​(p)−1superscriptsubscript𝜋𝑣1𝑝1\pi_{v}^{-1}(p)-1 inequalities of theform (1) are tight. Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery with unit prices, whereE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}). IfLP​-​U​(I,k)LP-U𝐼𝑘\mathrm{LP\hbox{-}U}(I,k) is infeasible or the cost of the solution exceeds k𝑘k,then we skip this value of k𝑘k. Let(x(v,j)OPT)v∈V,j∈[πv−1​(p)−1]subscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑥OPT𝑣𝑗formulae-sequence𝑣𝑉𝑗delimited-[]subscriptsuperscript𝜋1𝑣𝑝1(x^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{(v,j)})_{v\in V,j\in[\pi^{-1}_{v}(p)-1]} be thebasic optimal solution found.3.Let 𝐬𝐿𝑃superscript𝐬𝐿𝑃\mathbf{s}^{\mathit{LP}} be the shift action corresponding to(⌊x(v,j)OPT⌋)v∈V,j∈[πv−1​(p)−1]subscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑥OPT𝑣𝑗formulae-sequence𝑣𝑉𝑗delimited-[]subscriptsuperscript𝜋1𝑣𝑝1(\lfloor x^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{(v,j)}\rfloor)_{v\in V,j\in[\pi^{-1}_{v}(p)-1]}.(Note that the rounded solution indeed correctly describes a shiftaction and that its cost, i.e., the number of unit shifts itcontains, is at most k𝑘k.) Form a shift action 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} by extending𝐬𝐿𝑃superscript𝐬𝐿𝑃\mathbf{s}^{\mathit{LP}} so that it contains k+⌊k⌋𝑘𝑘k+\lfloor\sqrt{k}\rfloor unitshifts or, if this is impossible, then so that p𝑝p is on the topof every preference order. Since finding basic solutions for linear programs can be done inpolynomial time, the whole algorithm runs in polynomial time.Further, the cost of the computed shift action 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} is at mostOPT(I)+OPT(I)OPT𝐼OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+\sqrt{\operatorname*{OPT}(I)} unit shifts. To see this, consider thestep when the algorithm tries k=OPT(I)𝑘OPT𝐼k=\operatorname*{OPT}(I); if it terminatesearlier then our claim certainly is satisfied. For this value ofk𝑘k, LP​-​U​(I,k)LP-U𝐼𝑘\mathrm{LP\hbox{-}U}(I,k) certainly has a solution of cost at most k𝑘kbecause an optimal successful shift action for I𝐼I is one of itsfeasible solutions. Thus the algorithm terminates for this value ofk𝑘k and (in Step 3) outputs a shift actionwith at most OPT(I)+OPT(I)OPT𝐼OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+\sqrt{\operatorname*{OPT}(I)} unit shifts.

It remains to show that the computed shift action 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} issuccessful. If p𝑝p ends up at the top of every preference order,then surely 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} is a successful shift action. Let us consider thecase where 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} consists of exactly k+⌊k⌋𝑘𝑘k+\lfloor\sqrt{k}\rfloorunit shifts, i.e., where after applying 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s}, p𝑝p has scorescE​(p)+k+⌊k⌋subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+k+\lfloor\sqrt{k}\rfloor, for the value of k𝑘k forwhich the algorithm terminates. Since prior to applying 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s} eachcandidate c∉CBADk𝑐superscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘c\notin C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} had score at mostscE​(p)+k+⌊k⌋subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+k+\lfloor\sqrt{k}\rfloor, after applying 𝐬𝐬\mathbf{s}candidate p𝑝p certainly has score at least as high as theirs. Thus we onlyneed to show that each candidate in CBADksuperscriptsubscript𝐶BAD𝑘C_{\text{BAD}}^{k} also ends up with scoreat most scE​(p)+k+⌊k⌋subscriptsc𝐸𝑝𝑘𝑘\mathrm{sc}_{E}(p)+k+\lfloor\sqrt{k}\rfloor.

Using this algorithm and the combinatorial PTAS fromTheorem 4, we obtain our EPTAS (in short, if𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)<2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2} then we run the algorithm fromTheorem 4 and we run thealgorithm from Lemma 6 otherwise).

Proof.Given an instance I𝐼I of Borda-Shift-Bribery with unitprices, our algorithm proceeds as follows: If𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)<2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2} then it runs the algorithm fromTheorem 4 and, otherwise, if𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)⩾2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)\geqslant 2/\varepsilon^{2}, it runs the algorithm fromLemma 6. Then it returns the output fromthe respective algorithm.If 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)<2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2}, thenOPT(I)<2/ε2OPT𝐼2superscript𝜀2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2}, which means that the algorithm fromTheorem 4 runs in timeOPT(I)O​(1/ε)poly(|I|)=2O​(log⁡(1/ε)/ε)poly(|I|)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(1/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|)=2^{O(\log(1/\varepsilon)/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|).Since the algorithm from Lemma 6 alwaysruns in time poly​(|I|)poly𝐼\text{poly}(|I|), we conclude that the above algorithm runsin time 2O​(log⁡(1/ε)/ε)​poly​(|I|)superscript2𝑂1𝜀𝜀poly𝐼2^{O(\log(1/\varepsilon)/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|).When we run the algorithm from Theorem 4,the solution is always a (1+ε)1𝜀(1+\varepsilon)-approximation. On theother hand, when we invoke Lemma 6, we haveOPT(I)⩾𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2⩾1/ε2OPT𝐼𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼21superscript𝜀2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\geqslant\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2\geqslant 1/\varepsilon^{2}; hence, theoutput is of cost at mostOPT(I)+OPT(I)=(1+OPT(I)−0.5)OPT(I)⩽(1+ε)OPT(I)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+\sqrt{\operatorname*{OPT}(I)}=(1+\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{-0.5})\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}(I).This concludes the proof.□□\square

Given an instance I𝐼I of Borda-Shift-Bribery with unitprices, our algorithm proceeds as follows: If𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)<2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2} then it runs the algorithm fromTheorem 4 and, otherwise, if𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)⩾2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)\geqslant 2/\varepsilon^{2}, it runs the algorithm fromLemma 6. Then it returns the output fromthe respective algorithm.

If 𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)<2/ε2𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼2superscript𝜀2\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2}, thenOPT(I)<2/ε2OPT𝐼2superscript𝜀2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)<2/\varepsilon^{2}, which means that the algorithm fromTheorem 4 runs in timeOPT(I)O​(1/ε)poly(|I|)=2O​(log⁡(1/ε)/ε)poly(|I|)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{O(1/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|)=2^{O(\log(1/\varepsilon)/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|).Since the algorithm from Lemma 6 alwaysruns in time poly​(|I|)poly𝐼\text{poly}(|I|), we conclude that the above algorithm runsin time 2O​(log⁡(1/ε)/ε)​poly​(|I|)superscript2𝑂1𝜀𝜀poly𝐼2^{O(\log(1/\varepsilon)/\varepsilon)}\text{poly}(|I|).

When we run the algorithm from Theorem 4,the solution is always a (1+ε)1𝜀(1+\varepsilon)-approximation. On theother hand, when we invoke Lemma 6, we haveOPT(I)⩾𝑚𝑎𝑥​-​𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓​(I)/2⩾1/ε2OPT𝐼𝑚𝑎𝑥-𝑑𝑖𝑓𝑓𝐼21superscript𝜀2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\geqslant\mathit{max\hbox{-}diff}(I)/2\geqslant 1/\varepsilon^{2}; hence, theoutput is of cost at mostOPT(I)+OPT(I)=(1+OPT(I)−0.5)OPT(I)⩽(1+ε)OPT(I)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+\sqrt{\operatorname*{OPT}(I)}=(1+\operatorname*{OPT}(I)^{-0.5})\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(1+\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}(I).This concludes the proof.□□\square

Theorem 7 gives formal evidence thatapproximating Borda-Shift-Bribery is computationally easierfor the case of unit prices than for the general case or, even, forthe all-or-nothing prices case. The latter cases areW​[1]Wdelimited-[]1{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]-hard when parameterized by the budget [BCF+16] and thismeans that no EPTAS exists for them unless W​[1]=FPTWdelimited-[]1FPT{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]={{\mathrm{FPT}}}. If therewere an EPTAS for Borda-Shift-Bribery for the case of generalprices, or for the all-or-nothing prices, which ran in timef​(ε)​nO​(1)𝑓𝜀superscript𝑛𝑂1f(\varepsilon)n^{O(1)}, then we could plug inε=1/(2​B)𝜀12𝐵\varepsilon=1/(2B), where B𝐵B would be the budget limit, and solvethe problem exactly in time f​(12​B)​nO​(1)𝑓12𝐵superscript𝑛𝑂1f(\frac{1}{2B})n^{O(1)}, implying thatW​[1]=FPTWdelimited-[]1FPT{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]={{\mathrm{FPT}}}. Such connections betweenFPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} algorithms and EPTASes are well-known in theoretical computerscience [CT97], but, so far, have not found many applications incomputational social choice.

In addition to the above PTASes, we devise a simple greedy algorithmthat yields an asymptotic 1.5-approximation ratio for the special caseof uniform-all-or-nothing prices. While this will be subsumed by ourPTAS in the next section, the simplicity of the algorithm may make itmore practical than the LP-based PTAS inTheorem 12. The main idea of our greedyalgorithm is to simply shift the preferred candidate p𝑝p to the top inthe votes where p𝑝p is ranked lowest. The idea is formalized below.

Proof.Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery with uniform-all-or-nothing prices,where E=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}). Let m𝑚m be the number ofcandidates. When the lowest position is at least three, ties arebroken arbitrarily. However, if the lowest position is two, then thealgorithm breaks the ties by choosing the voter whose top-rankedcandidate has the highest score.For each j𝑗j, let Vj⊆Vsubscript𝑉𝑗𝑉V_{j}\subseteq V denote the set of the votersthat rank p𝑝p on position j𝑗j. Let I=(E,p,ψ)𝐼𝐸𝑝𝜓I=(E,p,\psi) be an instance ofBorda-Shift-Bribery with uniform-all-or-nothing prices,where E=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}). Let m𝑚m be the number ofcandidates. Recall that for all-or-nothing prices a solution issimply a set of bribed voters because we can move p𝑝p to the tops oftheir preference orders without additional cost.

Our algorithm proceed as follows: As long as p𝑝p is still not awinner, the algorithm bribes a voter that ranks p𝑝p on the lowestposition. When the lowest position is at least three, ties arebroken arbitrarily. However, if the lowest position is two, then thealgorithm breaks the ties by choosing the voter whose top-rankedcandidate has the highest score.

For each j𝑗j, let Vj⊆Vsubscript𝑉𝑗𝑉V_{j}\subseteq V denote the set of the votersthat rank p𝑝p on position j𝑗j. The key observation for the analysisof our algorithm is that to obtain an optimal solution, it sufficesto bribe all the voters from Vmsubscript𝑉𝑚V_{m}, then all the voters fromVm−1subscript𝑉𝑚1V_{m-1}, and so on, until some value i𝑖i, such that we only bribea subset of the voters from Visubscript𝑉𝑖V_{i}. We formalize this observation inthe following lemma.

Proof.Let W′superscript𝑊′W^{\prime} be some optimal solution for I𝐼I. As long as W′superscript𝑊′W^{\prime} isstill not in the required form, we modify W′superscript𝑊′W^{\prime} as follows:1.remove a voter in W′superscript𝑊′W^{\prime} that ranks p𝑝p on the highest position,2.add a voter from V∖W′𝑉superscript𝑊′V\setminus W^{\prime} that ranks p𝑝pon the lowest position.Note that in each step of this process the removed voter and theadded voter are from different Visubscript𝑉𝑖V_{i}’s. Thus in each step weincrease the final scores of some candidates by at most 111 (bycanceling a shift which decreased their scores) but we alsoincrease the final score of p𝑝p by at least 111. Hence W′superscript𝑊′W^{\prime}remains an optimal solution after each step. It is also easy tosee that the process terminates when a solution is in the requiredform. ⌟⌟\lrcorner

Clearly, the greedy algorithm also first bribes all the voters in(⋃j=i+1mVj)superscriptsubscript𝑗𝑖1𝑚subscript𝑉𝑗(\bigcup_{j=i+1}^{m}V_{j}) and it never bribes any voter in Vi−1subscript𝑉𝑖1V_{i-1}. Note that in E′superscript𝐸′E^{\prime} each voter ranks p𝑝p among hisor her top i𝑖i candidates.

Note that the greedy algorithm gives a solution of cost at mostii−1​OPT(I)+1𝑖𝑖1OPT𝐼1\frac{i}{i-1}\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+1 for i𝑖i defined inLemma 9. Hence, the worst guarantee, 1.5⋅OPT(I)+1⋅1.5OPT𝐼11.5\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)+1, is achieved for i=3𝑖3i=3.□□\square

We conclude this section by remarking that theBorda-Shift-Bribery problem withuniform-all-or-nothing prices was not known to be NP-hard before;specifically, Elkind et al. [EFS09] only showed NP-hardness for(1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices. Nevertheless, it is possible to(carefully) modify the proof to yield NP-hardness foruniform-all-or-nothing prices as well. We provide the full proof inAppendix A.

In this section we give our main result: A PTAS for the case ofShift-Bribery with an arbitrary positional scoring rule,whose scoring vectors are, possibly, different for different voters,and for arbitrary prices. The algorithm and the proof is somewhatinvolved and we split its description into two parts, by firstderiving an algorithm with an additive error and then using it to formthe desired PTAS.

The crucial part of our algorithmis an approximation algorithmthat yields a good solution in the case whereψmax​(I)superscript𝜓𝐼\psi^{\max}(I), the highest non-infinite price in the instance, is small.

The main complication in the general prices case, as opposed to theunit prices case, is that the cost of obtaining some k+1𝑘1k+1 points forthe preferred candidate can be far larger than the cost of obtainingk𝑘k points. Thus the main trick used in the proofs from the previoussection—deciding up front how many more points than in an optimalsolution the preferred candidate would get—cannot be directlyapplied. We work around this problem by first solving a linearprogram which, roughly speaking, for a given value ε>0𝜀0\varepsilon>0tells us how many extra points the preferred candidate needs to obtainso that he or she has score higher than all but at most1/ε1𝜀\nicefrac{{1}}{{\varepsilon}} candidates. Then, using a techniquesimilar to the one we used for Lemma 6—inparticular, solving a second linear program, for which a basicsolution contains a large number of integral variables—we find ourapproximate solution.

Now we use the approximation algorithm with additive error to derivean approximation algorithm with a purely multiplicative ratio. Theintuition behind this process is simple:Since the algorithm from Lemma 10 workswell when ψmax​(I)superscript𝜓𝐼\psi^{\max}(I) is small, we willfirst “preprocess” our instance so that ψmax​(I)superscript𝜓𝐼\psi^{\max}(I) is muchsmaller than OPT(I)OPT𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I). To do so, note that if we consider an optimalshift action 𝐬OPTsuperscript𝐬OPT\mathbf{s}^{\operatorname*{OPT}}, then there are only a few voters v𝑣v suchthat ψv​(svOPT)subscript𝜓𝑣subscriptsuperscript𝑠OPT𝑣\psi_{v}(s^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{v}) is large; specifically, for everyδ>0𝛿0\delta>0, only at most 1/δ1𝛿1/\delta voters haveψv​(svOPT)⩾δ​OPT(I)subscript𝜓𝑣subscriptsuperscript𝑠OPT𝑣𝛿OPT𝐼\psi_{v}(s^{\operatorname*{OPT}}_{v})\geqslant\delta\operatorname*{OPT}(I). This means that if we guesssuch voters and the numbers of unit shifts that we apply tothem, then we can reduce the instance I𝐼I to another instance I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime},where ψmax​(I′)superscript𝜓superscript𝐼′\psi^{\max}(I^{\prime}) is bounded by δ​OPT(I)𝛿OPT𝐼\delta\operatorname*{OPT}(I). We then runthe algorithm from Lemma 10 on I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime}.By selecting δ=O​(ε2)𝛿𝑂superscript𝜀2\delta=O(\varepsilon^{2}), the additiveerror becomesO​((δ/ε)​OPT(I))=O​(ε​OPT(I))𝑂𝛿𝜀OPT𝐼𝑂𝜀OPT𝐼O((\delta/\varepsilon)\operatorname*{OPT}(I))=O(\varepsilon\operatorname*{OPT}(I)) asintended.

We remark that as a corollary of Theorem 12, wealso get a PTAS for Borda-Shift-Bribery for arbitrary prices.

For the case of Copelandα family of rules, we show that theShift-Bribery problem is hard to approximate even for theunit prices and for the all-or-nothing prices. Specifically, we showthat an approximation algorithm for theCopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery implies an approximation algorithmfor the Densest-k𝑘k-Subgraph problem, which is believed to be hard to approximate [BCV+12].Below we describe our results generally, and then we provide theirproofs for respective types of price functions in the followingsections.

Although hardness of approximation of Dk𝑘kS within up to polynomialfactor is not known, inapproximability up to almost polynomial factoris known assuming the exponential time hypothesis (ETH) and itsgap version (Gap-ETH).555ETH [IP01, IPZ01] states that thereis no subexponential time algorithm that solves3SAT. Gap-ETH [Din16, MR17] states that no subexponentialtime algorithm can distinguish between a satisfiable 3CNFformula and one which is only (1−δ)1𝛿(1-\delta)-satisfiable for someabsolute constant δ>0𝛿0\delta>0. Specifically,Manurangsi [Man17] has shown that under the ETH assumption(the Gap-ETH assumption, respectively), Densest-k𝑘k-Subgraphis hard to approximate to within a factor ofn1/poly​(log⁡log⁡n)superscript𝑛1poly𝑛n^{1/\text{poly}(\log\log n)} (no​(1)superscript𝑛𝑜1n^{o(1)}, respectively). Together withTheorem 14, this implies the following corollary.

For the parameterization by the number of unit shifts, assuming Gap-ETHimplies that there is no FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} approximation scheme for the problemeven for the case of unit prices.

We are not aware of a constant factor FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} approximationalgorithms for the problem and it is possible that the factor 222above can be improved to larger constants, or even beyond aconstant. This remains an interesting open question.

Another parameter that has been considered in the literature is thenumber of affected voters. For this parameter, the exact version isknown to be W​[2]Wdelimited-[]2{{\mathrm{W}}}[2]-hard (for both unit prices and all-or-nothingprices) by a reduction from k𝑘k-Set Cover [BCF+16]. Due to therecent developments in parameterized inapproximability of k𝑘k-SetCover [CL16, CLM18], we can modify the W​[2]Wdelimited-[]2{{\mathrm{W}}}[2]-hardness proofto yield strong parameterized inapproximability results forCopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery with this parameter.

Notice that the hardness in Theorem 17 isstronger than Theorem 16 both in terms ofthe approximation ratio and that it requires weaker assumption.We also remark here that, for (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices,the optimum is exactly equal to the number of affected voters.Hence, the hardness above carries over to the “budget” parameter as well.To summarize, our results implies inapproximability foressentially all parameters left open by [BCF+16].

We need some additional notation in the following text. Let C𝐶C be aset of candidates. We write ⟨C⟩delimited-⟨⟩𝐶\left<C\right> to denote an arbitrary(but fixed) preference order over C𝐶C, and for each A⊆C𝐴𝐶A\subseteq C,by ⟨A⟩delimited-⟨⟩𝐴\left<A\right> we mean ⟨C⟩delimited-⟨⟩𝐶\left<C\right> restricted to thecandidates from A𝐴A. For example, for C={a,b,c,d,e}𝐶𝑎𝑏𝑐𝑑𝑒C=\{a,b,c,d,e\} andA={a,b,c}𝐴𝑎𝑏𝑐A=\{a,b,c\} we may write d≻⟨A⟩≻esucceeds𝑑delimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds𝑒d\succ\left<A\right>\succ e todenote preference order where d𝑑d is ranked ahead of all the membersof A𝐴A, and e𝑒e is below all members of A𝐴A (and below d𝑑d, ofcourse). When we use this notation to specify a preference order,typically the ranking of candidates within ⟨A⟩delimited-⟨⟩𝐴\left<A\right> isirrelevant for the argument. For each A⊆C𝐴𝐶A\subseteq C, we write⟨A⟩←←delimited-⟨⟩𝐴\overleftarrow{\left<A\right>}to denote the reverse of the order ⟨A⟩delimited-⟨⟩𝐴\left<A\right>.

In Definition 13 we have defined the optimization variantof the Densest-k𝑘k-Subgraph problem. In the decision variant,an instance consists of a graph G𝐺G and two positive integers, k𝑘k andt𝑡t, and we ask if it is possible to select k𝑘k vertices that jointlyinduce a graph with at least t𝑡t edges.

Given a graph G=(VG,EG)𝐺subscript𝑉𝐺subscript𝐸𝐺G=(V_{G},E_{G}) and a set of vertices U⊆VG𝑈subscript𝑉𝐺U\subseteq V_{G},by EG​[U]subscript𝐸𝐺delimited-[]𝑈E_{G}[U] we mean the set of edges from EGsubscript𝐸𝐺E_{G} that have bothendpoints in U𝑈U.

Often, whenconstructing hard instances for CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery, wewant to create additional voters and candidates so that the candidatesof our interest have certain scores and certain outcomes of pairwiseelections. The following proposition is especially useful for thispurpose; it is extracted from the proof of Theorem 4 of [BCF+16]with slight modifications. We provide a proof for the sake ofcompleteness.

Let S⊆B𝑆𝐵S\subseteq B be any subsets of B𝐵B of size a𝑎a. For ourelection E𝐸E, we create the following voters:1.One voter with preference order⟨A∪B⟩≻p≻dsucceedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝐴𝐵𝑝succeeds𝑑\left<A\cup B\right>\succ p\succ d.2.b𝑏b pairs of voters with preference orders⟨A⟩≻p≻⟨B⟩≻dsucceedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝐴𝑝succeedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝐵succeeds𝑑\left<A\right>\succ p\succ\left<B\right>\succ d andd≻⟨B⟩←≻⟨A⟩←≻psucceeds𝑑←delimited-⟨⟩𝐵succeeds←delimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds𝑝d\succ\overleftarrow{\left<B\right>}\succ\overleftarrow{\left<A\right>}\succ p.3.Two voters with preference ordersd≻⟨B⟩≻⟨A⟩≻psucceeds𝑑delimited-⟨⟩𝐵succeedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds𝑝d\succ\left<B\right>\succ\left<A\right>\succ p andp≻⟨A⟩←≻d≻⟨B⟩←succeeds𝑝←delimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds𝑑succeeds←delimited-⟨⟩𝐵p\succ\overleftarrow{\left<A\right>}\succ d\succ\overleftarrow{\left<B\right>}.4.Two voters with preference ordersp≻⟨B∖S⟩≻⟨S⟩≻⟨A⟩≻dsucceeds𝑝delimited-⟨⟩𝐵𝑆succeedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝑆succeedsdelimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds𝑑p\succ\left<B\setminus S\right>\succ\left<S\right>\succ\left<A\right>\succ dandd≻⟨A⟩←≻⟨S⟩←≻p≻⟨B∖S⟩←succeeds𝑑←delimited-⟨⟩𝐴succeeds←delimited-⟨⟩𝑆succeeds𝑝succeeds←delimited-⟨⟩𝐵𝑆d\succ\overleftarrow{\left<A\right>}\succ\overleftarrow{\left<S\right>}\succ p\succ\overleftarrow{\left<B\setminus S\right>}.5.For every candidate c∈A∪B𝑐𝐴𝐵c\in A\cup B, two voters withpreference orders half-seq1​(A∪B,c)≻p≻dsucceedshalf-seq1𝐴𝐵𝑐𝑝succeeds𝑑\text{half-seq1}(A\cup B,c)\succ p\succ d andd≻p≻half-seq2​(A∪B,c)succeeds𝑑𝑝succeedshalf-seq2𝐴𝐵𝑐d\succ p\succ\text{half-seq2}(A\cup B,c).It is simple to verify that (i) p𝑝p loses pairwise elections againstevery candidate in A𝐴A by 2​b+12𝑏12b+1 votes, (ii) p𝑝p wins pairwiseelections exactly against the candidates in the set{d}∪(B∖S)𝑑𝐵𝑆\{d\}\cup(B\setminus S), (iii) d𝑑d wins pairwise electionsexactly with the candidates from B𝐵B, and (iv) each candidate inA∪B𝐴𝐵A\cup B wins pairwise elections against exactly half of the othercandidates in A∪B𝐴𝐵A\cup B. These four features of E𝐸E indeed implythe claimed properties in the statement of the proposition.□□\square

The goal of this section is to prove Theorem 14 forthe case of all-or-nothing prices. (Soundness)Suppose contrapositivelythat OPT(I)⩽(k−1)/δOPT𝐼𝑘1𝛿\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(k-1)/\sqrt{\delta}. The unit prices part of Theorem 14 is established viaa reduction from the all-or-nothing price case; we state the reductionin the general form below as it will be used again in the nextsection.

Proof.Let the notation be as in the statement of the theorem and letE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}) be the election frominstance I𝐼I. We can now prove the desired inapproximability of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha} for unitprices, by simply applying Lemmas 19and 20 together with appropriate values of B𝐵B andB′superscript𝐵′B^{\prime}. This idea is formalized below.

Proof.Let (G,k,t)𝐺𝑘𝑡(G,k,t) be our input instance of Dk𝑘kS,where G=(VG,EG)𝐺subscript𝑉𝐺subscript𝐸𝐺G=(V_{G},E_{G}) is a graph, k𝑘kis the number of vertices we can select, and t𝑡tis the lower bound on the required number of edges in the graphinduced by the selected vertices. Let (G,k,t)𝐺𝑘𝑡(G,k,t) be our input instance of Dk𝑘kS,where G=(VG,EG)𝐺subscript𝑉𝐺subscript𝐸𝐺G=(V_{G},E_{G}) is a graph, k𝑘kis the number of vertices we can select, and t𝑡tis the lower bound on the required number of edges in the graphinduced by the selected vertices. In this section we show that approximatingCopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery is difficult even for FPTalgorithms, for the parameterizations by the number of unit shifts andby the number of affected voters.

We first show FPTFPT{{\mathrm{FPT}}} inapproximability ofCopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery with unit prices, parameterized bythe number of unit shifts (Theorem 16). To doso, let us recall the following hardness result regardingdistinguishing a graph with a k𝑘k-clique and one in which everyk𝑘k-vertex subgraph is sparse, as proved by Chalermsook etal. [CCK+17] (which, in turn, relies heavily on the reductionand the main lemma of Manurangsi [Man17]).

In Lemma 23, we give a reduction from theabove problem to CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery with unit prices,parameterized by the number of unit shifts (i.e., parameterized by thevalue of an optimal solution). Together withTheorem 22, this reduction impliesTheorem 16 (by selecting δ=ε/2𝛿𝜀2\delta=\varepsilon/2).

Before we proceed, we note that the reduction from the previoussection does not work in the parameterized context. The reason is thatthe optimum there depends on |VG|subscript𝑉𝐺|V_{G}|, whereas here we would like theoptimum to be bounded from above by some function of k𝑘k. Instead, weturn to the reduction used by Bredereck et al. [BCF+16] toprove W​[1]Wdelimited-[]1{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]-hardness of the problem; we only make slightmodifications to the reduction so that the analysis goes through evenfor the inapproximability proof. Once again, we describe thisreduction in two steps, by first reducing toCopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery with (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothingprices and then applying Lemma 20 to obtain aninstance with unit prices.

(Completeness) Suppose that there exists a set U⊆VG𝑈subscript𝑉𝐺U\subseteq V_{G} ofk𝑘k vertices that induces a k𝑘k-clique. Hence, p𝑝pis a winner of the election.

(Soundness) Suppose contrapositivelythat OPT(I)⩽(2−δ)​(k2)OPT𝐼2𝛿binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}. Now, let E∗⊆EGsuperscript𝐸subscript𝐸𝐺E^{*}\subseteq E_{G} denote the set of all edges e∈EG𝑒subscript𝐸𝐺e\in E_{G}such that at least one unit shift is applied to vesubscript𝑣𝑒v_{e} (i.e.,sve>0subscript𝑠subscript𝑣𝑒0s_{v_{e}}>0). We are ready to prove Lemma 23, which inturn gives Theorem 16.

Proof of Lemma 23.Let graph G=(VG,EG)𝐺subscript𝑉𝐺subscript𝐸𝐺G=(V_{G},E_{G}) and positive integer k𝑘k be our input.We first apply Lemma 24 to produce aninstance I𝐼I of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Briberywith (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices and, then, weinvoke Lemma 20 with B=⌈4/δ⌉𝐵4𝛿B=\lceil 4/\delta\rceil and B′=B​(|VG|4+1)superscript𝐵′𝐵superscriptsubscript𝑉𝐺41B^{\prime}=B(|V_{G}|^{4}+1) to produce an instance I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime} with unit price functions.The reduction runs in polynomial time.(Completeness) If G𝐺G contains a k𝑘k-clique, thenLemma 24 ensures thatOPT(I)⩽(k2)OPT𝐼binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant\binom{k}{2}. Moreover, Lemma 20then ensures thatOPT(I′)⩽(B+2)​OPT(I)⩽R​(k2)OPTsuperscript𝐼′𝐵2OPT𝐼𝑅binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\leqslant(B+2)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant R\binom{k}{2}, whereR=B+2=⌈4/δ⌉+2𝑅𝐵24𝛿2R=B+2=\lceil 4/\delta\rceil+2, as desired.(Soundness) If every k𝑘k-vertex subgraph of G𝐺G contains fewer thanδ​(k2)𝛿binomial𝑘2\delta\binom{k}{2} edges, then Lemma 24implies that OPT(I)>(2−δ)​(k2)OPT𝐼2𝛿binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)>(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}. Lemma 20 then ensures that:OPT(I′)OPTsuperscript𝐼′\displaystyle\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})⩾min⁡{B′,B⋅OPT(I)}>B​(2−δ)​(k2)absentsuperscript𝐵′⋅𝐵OPT𝐼𝐵2𝛿binomial𝑘2\displaystyle\geqslant\min\{B^{\prime},B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\}>B(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}⩾R​(1−δ/2)​(2−δ)​(k2)⩾R​(2−2​δ)​(k2).absent𝑅1𝛿22𝛿binomial𝑘2𝑅22𝛿binomial𝑘2\displaystyle\geqslant R\left(1-\delta/2\right)(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}\geqslant R(2-2\delta)\binom{k}{2}.This completes the proof.□□\square

Let graph G=(VG,EG)𝐺subscript𝑉𝐺subscript𝐸𝐺G=(V_{G},E_{G}) and positive integer k𝑘k be our input.We first apply Lemma 24 to produce aninstance I𝐼I of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Briberywith (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices and, then, weinvoke Lemma 20 with B=⌈4/δ⌉𝐵4𝛿B=\lceil 4/\delta\rceil and B′=B​(|VG|4+1)superscript𝐵′𝐵superscriptsubscript𝑉𝐺41B^{\prime}=B(|V_{G}|^{4}+1) to produce an instance I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime} with unit price functions.The reduction runs in polynomial time.

(Completeness) If G𝐺G contains a k𝑘k-clique, thenLemma 24 ensures thatOPT(I)⩽(k2)OPT𝐼binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant\binom{k}{2}. Moreover, Lemma 20then ensures thatOPT(I′)⩽(B+2)​OPT(I)⩽R​(k2)OPTsuperscript𝐼′𝐵2OPT𝐼𝑅binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\leqslant(B+2)\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant R\binom{k}{2}, whereR=B+2=⌈4/δ⌉+2𝑅𝐵24𝛿2R=B+2=\lceil 4/\delta\rceil+2, as desired.

(Soundness) If every k𝑘k-vertex subgraph of G𝐺G contains fewer thanδ​(k2)𝛿binomial𝑘2\delta\binom{k}{2} edges, then Lemma 24implies that OPT(I)>(2−δ)​(k2)OPT𝐼2𝛿binomial𝑘2\operatorname*{OPT}(I)>(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}. Lemma 20 then ensures that:OPT(I′)OPTsuperscript𝐼′\displaystyle\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})⩾min⁡{B′,B⋅OPT(I)}>B​(2−δ)​(k2)absentsuperscript𝐵′⋅𝐵OPT𝐼𝐵2𝛿binomial𝑘2\displaystyle\geqslant\min\{B^{\prime},B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\}>B(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}⩾R​(1−δ/2)​(2−δ)​(k2)⩾R​(2−2​δ)​(k2).absent𝑅1𝛿22𝛿binomial𝑘2𝑅22𝛿binomial𝑘2\displaystyle\geqslant R\left(1-\delta/2\right)(2-\delta)\binom{k}{2}\geqslant R(2-2\delta)\binom{k}{2}.This completes the proof.□□\square

We now move on to the parameterization by the number of affected voters(Theorem 17). Our result will rely onthe recent parameterized hardness of approximation result for Set Cover,due to Chen and Lin [CL16]. Before we state their result, letus briefly recall the (Minimum) Set Cover problem. An instance (U,𝒮)𝑈𝒮(U,\mathcal{S}) of Set Cover consists of the universe U={u1,…,uN}𝑈subscript𝑢1…subscript𝑢𝑁U=\{u_{1},\dots,u_{N}\}and a collection 𝒮={S1,…,SM}𝒮subscript𝑆1…subscript𝑆𝑀\mathcal{S}=\{S_{1},\dots,S_{M}\} of subsets of U𝑈U. Thegoal is to find a subcollection 𝒮′⊆𝒮superscript𝒮′𝒮\mathcal{S}^{\prime}\subseteq\mathcal{S} of smallest sizesuch that ⋃S∈𝒮′S=Usubscript𝑆superscript𝒮′𝑆𝑈\bigcup_{S\in\mathcal{S}^{\prime}}S=U. The result of Chen and Lin canbe stated as follows:

We note that the above result has been qualitatively strengthened byKarthik et al. [CLM18]. Indeed, if we were interested in gettinga super-constant hardness of approximation result, or a tightrunning-time lower bound, the latter would give a betterresult. However, we choose to state our hardness as simply as possible(i.e., as Theorem 17) and, hence,it suffices to start from the result of Chen and Lin [CL16].

Similarly to our previous results, we proveTheorem 17 in two steps. First, byreducing Set Cover to CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery with (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices, as stated below, and then by usingLemma 20.

Note that the above lemma, together with Theorem 25,immediately yields hardness inTheorem 17 for (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices. The proof ofLemma 26 is similar to the previous proofsexcept that now (some) voters correspond to the subsets and (some)candidates correspond to the elements of the universe; this can beseen as a modification of the W​[2]Wdelimited-[]2{{\mathrm{W}}}[2]-hardness proof of Bredereck etal. [BCF+16].

We are given an instance I~=(U,𝒮)~𝐼𝑈𝒮\tilde{I}=(U,\mathcal{S}) of Set Cover where U={u1,…,uN}𝑈subscript𝑢1…subscript𝑢𝑁U=\{u_{1},\dots,u_{N}\} and 𝒮={S1,…,SM}𝒮subscript𝑆1…subscript𝑆𝑀\mathcal{S}=\{S_{1},\dots,S_{M}\}. Let 𝒮′superscript𝒮′\mathcal{S}^{\prime} denote the collection of all subsets S𝑆Ssuch that at least one unit shift is applied to vSsubscript𝑣𝑆v_{S} (i.e. svS>0subscript𝑠subscript𝑣𝑆0s_{v_{S}}>0).Consider any element u∈U𝑢𝑈u\in U. Next we use Lemma 20 with appropriate values ofB𝐵B and B′superscript𝐵′B^{\prime} to translate the hardness from (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices to unit prices. The properties of thereduction from Set Cover to CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery withunit prices are summarized in the lemma below.

We remark that Lemma 27 immediately impliesthe hardness for unit prices stated inTheorem 17. This is because (i) implies thatthe reduction is FPT (i.e. the parameter in the new problem is no more thanthe previous parameter), and (ii) implies that, for any constant τ𝜏\tau,a τ𝜏\tau-approximation for OPT(I′)OPTsuperscript𝐼′\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime}) would give a (2​τ)2𝜏(2\tau)-approximation forOPT(I~)OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}); the latter is W​[1]Wdelimited-[]1{{\mathrm{W}}}[1]-hard (Theorem 25).

Proof of Lemma 27.Let an instance I~=(U,𝒮)~𝐼𝑈𝒮\tilde{I}=(U,\mathcal{S}) of Set Cover be our input.We first apply Lemma 26 to produce aninstance I=(E,ψ,p)𝐼𝐸𝜓𝑝I=(E,\psi,p) of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Briberywith (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices with electionE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}).We then invoke Lemma 20 with B=b⋅M+1𝐵⋅𝑏𝑀1B=b\cdot M+1 and B′=B⋅Msuperscript𝐵′⋅𝐵𝑀B^{\prime}=B\cdot M to producean instance I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime} with unit price functions.The reduction clearly runs in polynomial time.Moreover, notice that E𝐸E has width b𝑏b and b⩽N<|C|𝑏𝑁𝐶b\leqslant N<|C|.Since B>b⋅M⩾b⋅OPT(I)𝐵⋅𝑏𝑀⋅𝑏OPT𝐼B>b\cdot M\geqslant b\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I),Lemma 20 implies that any optimal successful shiftaffects at most OPT(I)=OPT(I~)OPT𝐼OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I)=\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}) voters as desired.Lemma 20 also yieldsOPT(I′)⩾min⁡{B′,B⋅OPT(I)}=B⋅OPT(I~)OPTsuperscript𝐼′superscript𝐵′⋅𝐵OPT𝐼⋅𝐵OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\geqslant\min\{B^{\prime},B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\}=B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}) andOPT(I′)⩽(B+b)⋅OPT(I)⩽(2​B)⋅OPT(I~)OPTsuperscript𝐼′⋅𝐵𝑏OPT𝐼⋅2𝐵OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\leqslant(B+b)\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(2B)\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}),which completes our proof.□□\square

Let an instance I~=(U,𝒮)~𝐼𝑈𝒮\tilde{I}=(U,\mathcal{S}) of Set Cover be our input.We first apply Lemma 26 to produce aninstance I=(E,ψ,p)𝐼𝐸𝜓𝑝I=(E,\psi,p) of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Briberywith (1,∞)1(1,\infty)-all-or-nothing prices with electionE=(C,V,{≻v}v∈V)𝐸𝐶𝑉subscriptsubscriptsucceeds𝑣𝑣𝑉E=(C,V,\{\succ_{v}\}_{v\in V}).We then invoke Lemma 20 with B=b⋅M+1𝐵⋅𝑏𝑀1B=b\cdot M+1 and B′=B⋅Msuperscript𝐵′⋅𝐵𝑀B^{\prime}=B\cdot M to producean instance I′superscript𝐼′I^{\prime} with unit price functions.

The reduction clearly runs in polynomial time.Moreover, notice that E𝐸E has width b𝑏b and b⩽N<|C|𝑏𝑁𝐶b\leqslant N<|C|.Since B>b⋅M⩾b⋅OPT(I)𝐵⋅𝑏𝑀⋅𝑏OPT𝐼B>b\cdot M\geqslant b\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I),Lemma 20 implies that any optimal successful shiftaffects at most OPT(I)=OPT(I~)OPT𝐼OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I)=\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}) voters as desired.

Lemma 20 also yieldsOPT(I′)⩾min⁡{B′,B⋅OPT(I)}=B⋅OPT(I~)OPTsuperscript𝐼′superscript𝐵′⋅𝐵OPT𝐼⋅𝐵OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\geqslant\min\{B^{\prime},B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\}=B\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}) andOPT(I′)⩽(B+b)⋅OPT(I)⩽(2​B)⋅OPT(I~)OPTsuperscript𝐼′⋅𝐵𝑏OPT𝐼⋅2𝐵OPT~𝐼\operatorname*{OPT}(I^{\prime})\leqslant(B+b)\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(I)\leqslant(2B)\cdot\operatorname*{OPT}(\tilde{I}),which completes our proof.□□\square

We have given the first PTAS for Shift-Bribery for the caseof positional scoring rules, and we have shown severe limitationsregarding approximability of CopelandαsuperscriptCopeland𝛼\text{Copeland}^{\alpha}-Shift-Bribery. Wehave also shown more efficient versions of our algorithms for the caseof the Borda rule with unit prices.Our PTAS improves upon the 222-approximation algorithm of Elkind etal. [EFS09, EF10], but their algorithm is quite robust and wasused, e.g., for combinatorial shift bribery [BFNT16] andbribery in approval elections [FST17]. It may be possible toapply our technique in these settings as well.

Another interesting direction is to see whether our ideas can beapplied to the Bribery problem, where the goal is to minimizethe number of bribed voters but we are allowed to change each bribedvoter’s preference arbitrarily. On this front, Keller etal. [KHH18] give a PTAS for the problem for Borda,t𝑡t-approval, and, more generally, any scoring rule that satisfies acertain technical condition. It remains open whether a PTAS existsfor all scoring rules.
